<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">This article is about the city in England. For other uses, see <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_(disambiguation)" class="mw-disambig" title="Nottingham (disambiguation)">Nottingham (disambiguation)</a>.</div>
<p class="mw-empty-elt">
</p>

<p><b>Nottingham</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/ɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;kit&#39;">ɪ</span><span title="/ŋ/: &#39;ng&#39; in &#39;sing&#39;">ŋ</span><span title="/ə/: &#39;a&#39; in &#39;about&#39;">ə</span><span title="&#39;m&#39; in &#39;my&#39;">m</span></span>/</a></span><small class="nowrap">&#160;(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:En-uk-Nottingham.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a>&#160;</span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/39/En-uk-Nottingham.ogg" class="internal" title="En-uk-Nottingham.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">NOT</span>-ing-əm</i></a>) is a <a href="/wiki/City_status_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="City status in the United Kingdom">city</a> and <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authorities_of_England" title="Unitary authorities of England">unitary authority area</a> in <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire" title="Nottinghamshire">Nottinghamshire</a>, England, 128 miles (206&#160;km) north of <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>, in the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands" title="East Midlands">East Midlands</a>.
</p><p>Nottingham has links to the legend of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a> and to the lace-making, bicycle (notably <a href="/wiki/Raleigh_Bicycle_Company" title="Raleigh Bicycle Company">Raleigh</a> bikes), and tobacco industries. It was granted its <a href="/wiki/City_charter" class="mw-redirect" title="City charter">city charter</a> in 1897 as part of <a href="/wiki/Queen_Victoria" title="Queen Victoria">Queen Victoria</a>'s Diamond Jubilee celebrations. Nottingham is a tourist destination; in 2011, visitors spent over £1.5&#160;billion—the thirteenth-highest amount in England's 111 <a href="/wiki/NUTS_of_the_United_Kingdom" class="mw-redirect" title="NUTS of the United Kingdom">statistical territories</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015, Nottingham had an estimated population of 321,550<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> with the wider <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Urban_Area" title="Nottingham Urban Area">urban area</a>, which includes many of the city's suburbs, having a population of 915,977. Its urban area is the largest in the east Midlands and the second-largest in the Midlands.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> The population of the Nottingham/<a href="/wiki/Derby" title="Derby">Derby</a> metropolitan area is estimated to be 1,610,000.<sup id="cite_ref-espon.eu_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-espon.eu-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_economy" title="Metropolitan economy">metropolitan economy</a> is the <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_economies_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="List of metropolitan economies in the United Kingdom">seventh largest in the United Kingdom</a> with a GDP of $50.9bn (2014).<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> The city is also ranked as a <a href="/wiki/Global_city#GaWC_study" title="Global city">sufficiency-level world city</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">Globalization and World Cities Research Network</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nottingham has an award-winning public transport system,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> including the largest publicly owned bus network in England<sup id="cite_ref-Our_Companies_–_NCT_–_Transdev_UK_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Our_Companies_–_NCT_–_Transdev_UK-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> and is also served by <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Nottingham railway station">Nottingham railway station</a> and the modern <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Express_Transit" title="Nottingham Express Transit">Nottingham Express Transit</a> tram system.
</p><p>It is also a major sporting centre, and in October 2015 was named 'Home of English Sport'.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/National_Ice_Centre" title="National Ice Centre">National Ice Centre</a>, <a href="/wiki/Holme_Pierrepont_National_Watersports_Centre" title="Holme Pierrepont National Watersports Centre">Holme Pierrepont National Watersports Centre</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Trent_Bridge" title="Trent Bridge">Trent Bridge</a> international cricket ground are all based in or around the city, which is also the home of two professional league football teams; the world's oldest professional league club <a href="/wiki/Notts_County" class="mw-redirect" title="Notts County">Notts County</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Nottingham Forest">Nottingham Forest</a>, famously two-time winners of the <a href="/wiki/UEFA_European_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="UEFA European Cup">UEFA European Cup</a> under <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough" title="Brian Clough">Brian Clough</a> in 1979 and 1980. The city also has professional rugby, ice hockey and cricket teams, and the Aegon <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Open" title="Nottingham Open">Nottingham Open</a>, an international tennis tournament on the ATP and WTA tours. This accolade came just over a year after Nottingham was named as the UK's first City of Football.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On 11 December 2015, Nottingham was named a "<a href="/wiki/City_of_Literature" title="City of Literature">City of Literature</a>" by <a href="/wiki/UNESCO" title="UNESCO">UNESCO</a>, joining Norwich, Melbourne, Prague and Barcelona as one of only a handful in the world.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The title reflects Nottingham's literary heritage, with <a href="/wiki/Lord_Byron" title="Lord Byron">Lord Byron</a>, <a href="/wiki/D._H._Lawrence" title="D. H. Lawrence">D. H. Lawrence</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alan_Sillitoe" title="Alan Sillitoe">Alan Sillitoe</a> having links to the city, as well as a strong contemporary literary community, a thriving publishing industry and a vibrant poetry scene.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It has two universities, the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">University of Nottingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Trent_University" title="Nottingham Trent University">Nottingham Trent University</a>, which are attended by over 60,000 students — University of Nottingham having &gt;33,000, and Nottingham Trent University having ~27,000, according to the respective university websites.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Nottingham" title="History of Nottingham">History of Nottingham</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Nottingham" title="Timeline of Nottingham">Timeline of Nottingham</a></div>
<p>The city predates <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxon</a> times and was known in <a href="/wiki/British_language_(Celtic)" class="mw-redirect" title="British language (Celtic)">Brythonic</a> as <i>Tigguo Cobauc</i>, meaning <i><a href="/wiki/City_of_Caves" title="City of Caves">Place of Caves</a></i> (known also as "City of Caves"). In modern Welsh it is known poetically as <i>Y Ty Ogofog</i> and Irish as <i>Na Tithe Uaimh</i> "The Cavey Dwelling".<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> When it fell under the rule of a Saxon chieftain named Snot it became known as "Snotingaham"; the homestead of Snot's people (<i>-inga</i> = the people of; <i>-ham</i> = homestead).<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Some authors derive "Nottingham" from <i>Snottenga</i>, caves, and <i>ham</i>, but "this has nothing to do with the English form".<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg/220px-Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg/330px-Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg/440px-Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="375" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nottingham_Castle_Gate_2009.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Castle" title="Nottingham Castle">Nottingham Castle</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Castle" title="Nottingham Castle">Nottingham Castle</a> was constructed in 1068 on a <a href="/wiki/Sandstone" title="Sandstone">sandstone</a> outcrop by the <a href="/wiki/River_Leen" title="River Leen">River Leen</a>. The Anglo-Saxon settlement was originally confined to the area today known as the Lace Market and was surrounded by a substantial defensive ditch and rampart, which fell out of use following the Norman Conquest and was filled by the time of the Domesday Survey (1086).<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_C._Lomax_2013_pp._83_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_C._Lomax_2013_pp._83-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Following the Norman Conquest the Saxon settlement developed into the English <a href="/wiki/Borough" title="Borough">Borough</a> of Nottingham and housed a Town Hall and Law Courts. A settlement also developed around the castle on the hill opposite and was the French borough supporting the Normans in the castle. Eventually, the space between was built on as the town grew and the <a href="/wiki/Old_Market_Square" title="Old Market Square">Old Market Square</a> became the focus of Nottingham several centuries later.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_C._Lomax_2013_pp._83_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_C._Lomax_2013_pp._83-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> Defences, consisted initially of a ditch and bank in the early 12th century. The ditch was later widened, in the mid-13th century, and a stone wall built around much of the perimeter of the town. A short length of the wall survives, and is visible at the northern end of Maid Marian Way, and is protected as a Scheduled Monument.<sup id="cite_ref-Scott_C._Lomax_2013_pp._83_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Scott_C._Lomax_2013_pp._83-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>On the return of <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard the Lionheart</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Crusades</a>, the castle was occupied by supporters of <a href="/wiki/John_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="John of England">Prince John</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Sheriff_of_Nottingham_(position)" title="Sheriff of Nottingham (position)">Sheriff of Nottingham</a>. It was besieged by Richard and, after a sharp conflict, was captured.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> In the legends of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>, Nottingham Castle is the scene of the final showdown between the Sheriff and the hero outlaw.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg/220px-Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg" width="220" height="107" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg/330px-Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg/440px-Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4500" data-file-height="2196" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Siberechts_-_View_of_Nottingham_from_the_East.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nottingham from the east, c. 1695, painted by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Siberechts" title="Jan Siberechts">Jan Siberechts</a></div></div></div>
<p>By the 15th century Nottingham had established itself as a centre of a thriving export trade in religious sculpture made from <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Alabaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Nottingham Alabaster">Nottingham Alabaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> The town became a <a href="/wiki/County_corporate" title="County corporate">county corporate</a> in 1449<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> giving it effective self-government, in the words of the charter, "for eternity". The Castle and Shire Hall were expressly excluded and remained as detached Parishes of <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire" title="Nottinghamshire">Nottinghamshire</a>.
</p><p>One of those highly impressed by Nottingham in the late 18th century was the German traveller <a href="/wiki/Karl_Philipp_Moritz" title="Karl Philipp Moritz">C. P. Moritz</a>, who wrote in 1782, "Of all the towns I have seen outside London, Nottingham is the loveliest and neatest. Everything had a modern look, and a large space in the centre was hardly less handsome than a London square. A charming footpath leads over the fields to the highway, where a bridge spans the Trent. … Nottingham … with its high houses, red roofs and church steeples, looks excellent from a distance."<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">Industrial Revolution</a>, much of Nottingham's prosperity was founded on the <a href="/wiki/Textile_manufacture_during_the_Industrial_Revolution" title="Textile manufacture during the Industrial Revolution">textile industry</a>; in particular, the city became an internationally important centre of <a href="/wiki/Lace" title="Lace">lace</a> manufacture. In 1831 citizens rioted in protest against the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Newcastle" title="Duke of Newcastle">Duke of Newcastle</a>'s opposition to the <a href="/wiki/Reform_Act_1832" title="Reform Act 1832">Reform Act 1832</a>, setting fire to his residence, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Castle" title="Nottingham Castle">Nottingham Castle</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg/220px-Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg" width="220" height="228" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg/330px-Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg/440px-Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg 2x" data-file-width="6850" data-file-height="7087" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Nottingham_Map_1831_by_Staveley_and_Wood.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nottingham in 1831</div></div></div>
<p>In common with the UK textile industry, Nottingham's textile sector fell into decline in the decades following World War II.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>  Little textile manufacture now takes place in Nottingham; however, many of the former industrial buildings in the Lace Market district have been restored and put to new uses.
</p><p>Nottingham was one of the boroughs reformed by the <a href="/wiki/Municipal_Corporations_Act_1835" title="Municipal Corporations Act 1835">Municipal Corporations Act 1835</a>, and at that time consisted of the parishes of St Mary, St Nicholas and St Peter. It was expanded in 1877 by adding the parishes of <a href="/wiki/Basford,_Nottingham" title="Basford, Nottingham">Basford</a>, Brewhouse Yard, <a href="/wiki/Bulwell" title="Bulwell">Bulwell</a>, <a href="/wiki/Radford,_Nottingham" title="Radford, Nottingham">Radford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sneinton" title="Sneinton">Sneinton</a>, Standard Hill, and parts of the parishes of <a href="/wiki/West_Bridgford" title="West Bridgford">West Bridgford</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carlton,_Nottinghamshire" title="Carlton, Nottinghamshire">Carlton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilford,_Nottinghamshire" class="mw-redirect" title="Wilford, Nottinghamshire">Wilford</a> (North Wilford). In 1889 Nottingham became a <a href="/wiki/County_borough" title="County borough">county borough</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Local_Government_Act_1888" title="Local Government Act 1888">Local Government Act 1888</a>. City status was awarded as part of the Diamond Jubilee celebrations of Queen Victoria, being signified in a letter from the prime minister, the <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cecil,_3rd_Marquess_of_Salisbury" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Cecil, 3rd Marquess of Salisbury">Marquess of Salisbury</a> to the mayor, dated 18 June 1897. Nottingham was extended in 1933 by adding <a href="/wiki/Bilborough" title="Bilborough">Bilborough</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wollaton" title="Wollaton">Wollaton</a>, parts of the parishes of Bestwood Park and <a href="/wiki/Colwick" title="Colwick">Colwick</a>, and a recently developed part of the <a href="/wiki/Beeston_Urban_District" title="Beeston Urban District">Beeston Urban District</a>. A further boundary extension was granted in 1951 when Clifton and Wilford (south of the River Trent) were incorporated into the city.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-localhist_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-localhist-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="toccolours" style="width:30em;border-spacing: 0;margin:0.5em auto;"><tbody><tr><th class="navbox-title" colspan="2" style="padding:0.25em;font-size:110%">Demographic evolution of Nottingham</th></tr><tr valign="top"><td style="padding:0 0.5em"><table style="border-spacing:0;width:15em"><tbody><tr style="font-size:95%"><th style="border-bottom:1px solid black;padding:1px;width:auto">Year</th><th style="border-bottom:1px solid black;padding:1px 2px;text-align:right"><abbr title="Population">Pop.</abbr></th></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">4th century</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">&lt;37</td></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">10th century</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">&lt;1,000</td></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">11th century</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">1,500</td></tr></tbody></table></td><td style="padding:0 0.5em;border-left:solid 1px #aaa"><table style="border-spacing:0;width:15em"><tbody><tr style="font-size:95%"><th style="border-bottom:1px solid black;padding:1px;width:auto">Year</th><th style="border-bottom:1px solid black;padding:1px 2px;text-align:right"><abbr title="Population">Pop.</abbr></th></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">14th century</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">3,000</td></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px;border-bottom:1px solid #bbbbbb">Early 17th century</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px;border-bottom:1px solid #bbbbbb">4,000</td></tr><tr><th style="text-align:center;padding:1px">Late 17th century</th><td style="text-align:right;padding:1px">5,000</td></tr></tbody></table></td></tr></tbody></table>

<p>Electric trams were introduced to the city in 1901; they served the city for 35 years until 1936. Trams were reintroduced after 68 years when <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Express_Transit" title="Nottingham Express Transit">a new network</a> opened in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-localhist_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-localhist-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In the sporting world, Nottingham is home to the world's oldest professional football club, <a href="/wiki/Notts_County_F.C." title="Notts County F.C.">Notts County</a>, which was formed in 1862. The town's other football club, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Forest_F.C." title="Nottingham Forest F.C.">Nottingham Forest</a>, had a period of success between 1977 and 1993 under manager <a href="/wiki/Brian_Clough" title="Brian Clough">Brian Clough</a>, winning the <a href="/wiki/Football_League_First_Division" title="Football League First Division">First Division</a>, four <a href="/wiki/Football_League_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="Football League Cup">League Cups</a>, a <a href="/wiki/UEFA_Super_Cup" title="UEFA Super Cup">UEFA Super Cup</a> and two <a href="/wiki/European_Cup" class="mw-redirect" title="European Cup">European Cups</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> During this time Forest signed <a href="/wiki/Trevor_Francis" title="Trevor Francis">Trevor Francis</a>, Britain's first £1&#160;million footballer, who joined the club in February 1979 from <a href="/wiki/Birmingham_City_F.C." title="Birmingham City F.C.">Birmingham City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The city was the site of race riots in 1958, centred on the <a href="/wiki/St_Ann%27s,_Nottingham" title="St Ann&#39;s, Nottingham">St Ann's</a> neighbourhood.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>During the second half of the 20th century Nottingham saw urban growth with the development of new public and private housing estates and new urban centres, which have engulfed former rural villages such as Bilborough, Wollaton, Gedling and Bramcote. South of the river there has also been expansion with new areas such as Edwalton and West Bridgford, adding to Nottingham's <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">urban sprawl</a>. Although this growth slowed towards the end of the century, the modern pressures for more affordable and council housing is back on the political agenda and there is now pressure on the <a href="/wiki/Green_Belt" class="mw-redirect" title="Green Belt">Green Belt</a> which surrounds the city.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government">Government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Local_government">Local government</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Local government">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Council-House-Nottingham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Council-House-Nottingham.jpg/220px-Council-House-Nottingham.jpg" width="220" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Council-House-Nottingham.jpg/330px-Council-House-Nottingham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/90/Council-House-Nottingham.jpg/440px-Council-House-Nottingham.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2320" data-file-height="1944" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Council-House-Nottingham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Council_House" title="Nottingham Council House">Nottingham Council House</a></div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Nottingham_City_Council" title="Nottingham City Council">Nottingham City Council</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Unitary_authority" title="Unitary authority">unitary authority</a> based at <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Council_House" title="Nottingham Council House">Nottingham Council House</a> in <a href="/wiki/Old_Market_Square" title="Old Market Square">Old Market Square</a>. It consists of 55 <a href="/wiki/Councillors" class="mw-redirect" title="Councillors">councillors</a>, representing 20 <a href="/wiki/Ward_(politics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ward (politics)">wards</a>, who are elected every four years; the last elections being held on <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_City_Council_election,_2015" title="Nottingham City Council election, 2015">7 May 2015</a>.
</p><p>The city also has a <a href="/wiki/Lord_Mayor_of_Nottingham" class="mw-redirect" title="Lord Mayor of Nottingham">Lord Mayor</a> who is selected by city councillors from among themselves. The position is ceremonial and has no formal power or authority.
</p><p>The City of Nottingham's boundaries are tightly drawn and exclude several suburbs and satellite towns that are usually considered part of <a href="/wiki/Greater_Nottingham" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Nottingham">Greater Nottingham</a>. The western suburbs of <a href="/wiki/Beeston,_Nottinghamshire" title="Beeston, Nottinghamshire">Beeston</a>, <a href="/wiki/Stapleford,_Nottinghamshire" title="Stapleford, Nottinghamshire">Stapleford</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eastwood,_Nottinghamshire" title="Eastwood, Nottinghamshire">Eastwood</a> are administered by <a href="/wiki/Broxtowe" title="Broxtowe">Broxtowe borough council</a>. Further west still, the Nottingham urban district extends into <a href="/wiki/Derbyshire" title="Derbyshire">Derbyshire</a> where <a href="/wiki/Ilkeston" title="Ilkeston">Ilkeston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Long_Eaton" title="Long Eaton">Long Eaton</a> are administered by <a href="/wiki/Erewash" title="Erewash">Erewash borough council</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ripley,_Derbyshire" title="Ripley, Derbyshire">Ripley</a> by <a href="/wiki/Amber_Valley" title="Amber Valley">Amber Valley</a>. To the north, <a href="/wiki/Hucknall" title="Hucknall">Hucknall</a> is controlled by <a href="/wiki/Ashfield,_Nottinghamshire" title="Ashfield, Nottinghamshire">Ashfield district council</a>, while in the east <a href="/wiki/Arnold,_Nottinghamshire" title="Arnold, Nottinghamshire">Arnold</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carlton,_Nottinghamshire" title="Carlton, Nottinghamshire">Carlton</a> form part of the <a href="/wiki/Borough_of_Gedling" title="Borough of Gedling">Borough of Gedling</a>. South of the river, the suburb of <a href="/wiki/West_Bridgford,_Nottinghamshire" class="mw-redirect" title="West Bridgford, Nottinghamshire">West Bridgford</a> lies in <a href="/wiki/Rushcliffe" title="Rushcliffe">Rushcliffe</a>, as do the outlying villages of <a href="/wiki/Ruddington" title="Ruddington">Ruddington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tollerton,_Nottinghamshire" title="Tollerton, Nottinghamshire">Tollerton</a> and the town of <a href="/wiki/Bingham,_Nottinghamshire" title="Bingham, Nottinghamshire">Bingham</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GreaterNottingham-map.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/GreaterNottingham-map.png/220px-GreaterNottingham-map.png" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/GreaterNottingham-map.png/330px-GreaterNottingham-map.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/GreaterNottingham-map.png/440px-GreaterNottingham-map.png 2x" data-file-width="2443" data-file-height="1600" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GreaterNottingham-map.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Map illustrating the boundaries of the city and the wider <a href="/wiki/Greater_Nottingham" class="mw-redirect" title="Greater Nottingham">Greater Nottingham</a> area</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="UK_Parliament">UK Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: UK Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nottingham has three UK parliamentary <a href="/wiki/Constituency" class="mw-redirect" title="Constituency">constituency</a> seats within its boundaries. <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_North_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Nottingham North (UK Parliament constituency)">Nottingham North</a> has been represented since 2017 by <a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a> <a href="/wiki/Member_of_Parliament" class="mw-redirect" title="Member of Parliament">MP</a> <a href="/wiki/Alex_Norris" title="Alex Norris">Alex Norris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_East_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Nottingham East (UK Parliament constituency)">Nottingham East</a> since 2010 by Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Chris_Leslie_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chris Leslie (politician)">Chris Leslie</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_South_(UK_Parliament_constituency)" title="Nottingham South (UK Parliament constituency)">Nottingham South</a> since 2010 by Labour MP <a href="/wiki/Lilian_Greenwood" title="Lilian Greenwood">Lilian Greenwood</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="European_Parliament">European Parliament</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: European Parliament">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Nottingham lies within the <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_(European_Parliament_constituency)" title="East Midlands (European Parliament constituency)">East Midlands</a> European parliamentary constituency. In 2014, it elected five <a href="/wiki/Member_of_the_European_Parliament" title="Member of the European Parliament">MEPs</a>: <a href="/wiki/Margot_Parker" title="Margot Parker">Margot Parker</a> (<a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UKIP</a>), <a href="/wiki/Roger_Helmer" title="Roger Helmer">Roger Helmer</a> (<a href="/wiki/UK_Independence_Party" title="UK Independence Party">UKIP</a>), <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Lewer" title="Andrew Lewer">Andrew Lewer</a> (<a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>), <a href="/wiki/Emma_McClarkin" title="Emma McClarkin">Emma McClarkin</a> (<a href="/wiki/Conservative_Party_(UK)" title="Conservative Party (UK)">Conservative</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Glenis_Willmott" title="Glenis Willmott">Glenis Willmott</a> (<a href="/wiki/Labour_Party_(UK)" title="Labour Party (UK)">Labour</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Emergency services are provided by <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire_Police" title="Nottinghamshire Police">Nottinghamshire Police</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nottinghamshire_Fire_and_Rescue_Service" title="Nottinghamshire Fire and Rescue Service">Nottinghamshire Fire and Rescue Service</a> and <a href="/wiki/East_Midlands_Ambulance_Service" title="East Midlands Ambulance Service">East Midlands Ambulance Service</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Nottingham is situated on an area of low hills<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> along the lower valley of the <a href="/wiki/River_Trent" title="River Trent">River Trent</a>, and is surrounded by the <a href="/wiki/Sherwood_Forest" title="Sherwood Forest">Sherwood Forest</a> in the north, the <a href="/wiki/South_Yorkshire_Coalfield" title="South Yorkshire Coalfield">Nottinghamshire, Derbyshire and Yorkshire Coalfield</a> in the west, and the <a href="/wiki/Vale_of_Belvoir" title="Vale of Belvoir">Trent and Belvoir Vales</a> in the east and south.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Like the rest of the United Kingdom, Nottingham has a <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">temperate oceanic climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen</a>: Cfb) and experiences warm mild summers and mild to cool winters with abundant precipitation throughout the year. There are weather-reporting stations close to Nottingham—the former "Nottingham Weather Centre", at <a href="/wiki/Watnall" title="Watnall">Watnall</a>, about 6 miles (10&#160;km) north-west of the city centre; and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">University of Nottingham</a>'s agricultural campus at <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Bonington" title="Sutton Bonington">Sutton Bonington</a>, about 10 miles (16&#160;km) to the south-west of the city centre. The highest temperature recorded in Nottingham (Watnall) stands at 34.6&#160;°C (94.3&#160;°F),<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> whilst at Sutton Bonington stands at 34.8&#160;°C (94.6&#160;°F)<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> both recorded on <a href="/wiki/1990_United_Kingdom_heat_wave" title="1990 United Kingdom heat wave">3 August 1990</a>, and the record-high minimum temperature is 19.9&#160;°C (67.8&#160;°F)<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> recorded in August 2004. On average, a temperature of 25&#160;°C (77&#160;°F) or above is recorded on 11.0 days per year<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> at Watnall (1981–2010), and the warmest day of the year reaches an average of 29.4&#160;°C (84.9&#160;°F).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>For the period 1981–2010 Nottingham (Watnall) recorded on average 42.9 days of air frost per year,<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> and Sutton Bonington 47.1.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> The lowest recorded temperature in Nottingham (Watnall) is −13.3&#160;°C (8.1&#160;°F) recorded in <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_1962%E2%80%9363_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Winter of 1962–63 in the United Kingdom">January 1963</a><sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/January_1987_Southeast_England_snowfall" class="mw-redirect" title="January 1987 Southeast England snowfall">January 1987</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> The record-low maximum temperature is −6.3&#160;°C (20.7&#160;°F)<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> recorded in <a href="/wiki/Winter_of_1962%E2%80%9363_in_the_United_Kingdom" title="Winter of 1962–63 in the United Kingdom">January 1963</a>. For the period of 1981–2010, the coldest temperature of the year reaches an average of 
−6.6&#160;°C (20.1&#160;°F)<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> in Nottingham (Watnall).
</p><p><br />
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_quality">Air quality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Air quality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 2017 it was reported that Nottingham is one of a number of UK cities that break WHO air pollution guidelines for the maximum concentration of small particulate matter. Pollution in part being caused by harmful wood-burning stoves.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Green_Belt">Green Belt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Green Belt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_and_Derby_Green_Belt" title="Nottingham and Derby Green Belt">Nottingham and Derby Green Belt</a></div>
<p>Nottingham is bounded by a green belt area, provisionally drawn up from the 1950s. Completely encircling the city, it extends for several miles into the surrounding districts, as well as towards Derby.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The geographical centre of Nottingham is usually defined as the <a href="/wiki/Old_Market_Square" title="Old Market Square">Old Market Square</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The square is dominated by the <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Council_House" title="Nottingham Council House">Council House</a>, which replaced the Nottingham Exchange Building, built in 1726. The Council House was built in the 1920s to display civic pride, ostentatiously using <a href="/wiki/Baroque" title="Baroque">baroque</a> columns and placing stone statues of two lions at the front to stand watch over the square. The <a href="/wiki/Exchange_Arcade" class="mw-redirect" title="Exchange Arcade">Exchange Arcade</a>, on the ground floor, is an upmarket shopping centre containing boutiques.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Byron_house_2.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Byron_house_2.JPG/220px-Byron_house_2.JPG" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Byron_house_2.JPG/330px-Byron_house_2.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Byron_house_2.JPG/440px-Byron_house_2.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2913" data-file-height="1949" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Byron_house_2.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Nottingham Trent University, Arkwright Building</div></div></div>
<p>Tall office buildings line Maid Marian Way. The Georgian area around Oxford and Regent Streets is dominated by small professional firms. The <a href="/wiki/Albert_Hall,_Nottingham" title="Albert Hall, Nottingham">Albert Hall</a> faces the <a href="/wiki/Gothic_revival_architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="Gothic revival architecture">Gothic revival</a> <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Cathedral" title="Nottingham Cathedral">St Barnabas' Roman Catholic Cathedral</a> by <a href="/wiki/Augustus_Welby_Northmore_Pugin" class="mw-redirect" title="Augustus Welby Northmore Pugin">Pugin</a>. <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Castle" title="Nottingham Castle">Nottingham Castle</a> and its grounds are located further south in the western third of the city. The central third descends from the University district in the north, past <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Trent_University" title="Nottingham Trent University">Nottingham Trent University</a>'s Gothic revival Arkwright Building. The university also owns many other buildings in this area. The Theatre Royal on Theatre Square, with its pillared façade, was built in 1865. King and Queen Streets are home to striking <a href="/wiki/Victorian_architecture" title="Victorian architecture">Victorian</a> buildings designed by such architects as <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Waterhouse" title="Alfred Waterhouse">Alfred Waterhouse</a> and <a href="/wiki/Watson_Fothergill" title="Watson Fothergill">Watson Fothergill</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>To the south, is <a href="/wiki/Broadmarsh_Shopping_Centre" class="mw-redirect" title="Broadmarsh Shopping Centre">Broadmarsh Shopping Centre</a>. The Canal-side further south of this is adjacent to <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_railway_station" class="mw-redirect" title="Nottingham railway station">Nottingham railway station</a> and home to numerous redeveloped 19th-century industrial buildings, reused as bars and restaurants.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>The eastern third of the city centre contains the Victoria Shopping Centre, built in the 1970s on the site of the demolished Victoria Railway Station. All that remains of the old station is the clock tower and the station hotel, now the Nottingham Hilton Hotel. The 250-foot-high Victoria Centre flats stand above the shopping centre and are the tallest buildings in the city. The eastern third contains <a href="/wiki/Hockley_Village" class="mw-redirect" title="Hockley Village">Hockley Village</a>. Hockley is where many of Nottingham's unique, independent shops are to be found. It is also home to two alternative cinemas.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lace_Market">Lace Market</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Lace Market">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG/220px-Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG" width="220" height="158" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG/330px-Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG/440px-Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2769" data-file-height="1993" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lace_market_justice_galleries.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Galleries of Justice in the Lace Market</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Lace_Market" title="Lace Market">Lace Market</a> area just south of Hockley has streets with four to seven-storey red brick warehouses, iron railings and red phone boxes.
</p><p>Buildings have been converted into apartments, bars and restaurants. <a href="/wiki/The_Adams_Building_Nottingham" class="mw-redirect" title="The Adams Building Nottingham">Adams Building</a>, built by Thomas Chambers Hine for Thomas Adams (1817–1873), is currently used by <a href="/wiki/New_College_Nottingham" class="mw-redirect" title="New College Nottingham">New College Nottingham</a>. The Georgian-built Shire Hall is home to the <a href="/wiki/Galleries_of_Justice" class="mw-redirect" title="Galleries of Justice">Galleries of Justice</a> and was Nottingham's main court and prison building.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pubs">Pubs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Pubs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Ye_Olde_Trip_To_Jerusalem" class="mw-redirect" title="Ye Olde Trip To Jerusalem">Ye Olde Trip To Jerusalem</a></i> (the <i>Trip</i>), partially built into the cave system beneath Nottingham Castle, is a contender for the title of England's Oldest Pub, as it is supposed to have been established in 1189.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/The_Bell_Inn" title="The Bell Inn">The Bell Inn</a></i> in the Old Market Square, and <i><a href="/wiki/Ye_Olde_Salutation_Inn" title="Ye Olde Salutation Inn">Ye Olde Salutation Inn</a></i> (the <i>Salutation</i>) in Maid Marian Way have both disputed this claim. The <i>Trip</i>'s current timber building probably dates back to the 17th or 18th century, but the caves are certainly older and may have been used to store beer and water for the castle during medieval times. There are also caves beneath the <i>Salutation</i> that date back to the medieval period, although they are no longer used as beer cellars. The <i>Bell Inn</i> is probably the oldest of the three pub buildings still standing, according to <a href="/wiki/Dendrochronology" title="Dendrochronology">dendrochronology</a>, and has medieval cellars that are still used to store beer.<sup id="cite_ref-Lomax2013_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lomax2013-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NHS_Front.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/NHS_Front.jpg/220px-NHS_Front.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/6d/NHS_Front.jpg/330px-NHS_Front.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/6/6d/NHS_Front.jpg 2x" data-file-width="380" data-file-height="285" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NHS_Front.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The south side of <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_High_School" title="Nottingham High School">Nottingham High School</a></div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Education_in_Nottingham" title="Education in Nottingham">Education in Nottingham</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_schools_in_Nottingham" title="List of schools in Nottingham">List of schools in Nottingham</a></div>
<p>Over 61,000 students attend the city's two universities, <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Trent_University" title="Nottingham Trent University">Nottingham Trent University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham" title="University of Nottingham">University of Nottingham</a>, both of which have several campuses in the city. In 2011/12, Nottingham Trent University had 27,930 students, and the University of Nottingham had 35,630.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Nottingham_Medical_School" title="University of Nottingham Medical School">University of Nottingham Medical School</a> is part of the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Medical_Centre" title="Queen&#39;s Medical Centre">Queen's Medical Centre</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Four <a href="/wiki/Further_education" title="Further education">further education</a> colleges are located in Nottingham. <a href="/wiki/Bilborough_College" title="Bilborough College">Bilborough College</a> is solely a <a href="/wiki/Sixth_form" title="Sixth form">sixth-form</a> college. <a href="/wiki/Central_College_Nottingham" title="Central College Nottingham">Central College</a> was formed from the merger of South Nottingham College and Castle College. <a href="/wiki/New_College_Nottingham" class="mw-redirect" title="New College Nottingham">New College</a> was formed from a merger of four smaller further education colleges.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>. The <a href="/wiki/Confetti_Institute_of_Creative_Technologies" title="Confetti Institute of Creative Technologies">Confetti Institute of Creative Technologies</a> is a further education college that specialises in media, and is owned by Nottingham Trent University.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Nottingham also has dozens of sixth-form colleges and academies that provide education and training for adults aged over sixteen.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nottingham also has a number of independent schools, with <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_High_School" title="Nottingham High School">Nottingham High School</a>—which was founded in 1513<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>—being the city's oldest educational establishment.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy">Economy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Nottingham&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Economy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HMRC_Nottingham.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HMRC_Nottingham.jpg/220px-HMRC_Nottingham.jpg" width="220" height="80" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HMRC_Nottingham.jpg/330px-HMRC_Nottingham.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/HMRC_Nottingham.jpg/440px-HMRC_Nottingham.jpg 2x" data-file-width="923" data-file-height="334" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HMRC_Nottingham.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Part of the <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HMRC</a> <a href="/wiki/Castle_Meadow_Campus" title="Castle Meadow Campus">Castle Meadow Campus</a> in Nottingham</div></div></div>
<p>In 2010, Nottingham City Council announced that the target sectors of their economic development strategy would include low-carbon technologies; <a href="/wiki/Digital_media" title="Digital media">digital media</a>; <a href="/wiki/Life_sciences" class="mw-redirect" title="Life sciences">life sciences</a>; financial and business services; and retail and leisure.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Nottingham is home to the headquarters of several companies. These include <a href="/wiki/Alliance_Boots" title="Alliance Boots">Alliance Boots</a> (formerly <a href="/wiki/Boots_the_Chemists" class="mw-redirect" title="Boots the Chemists">Boots the Chemists</a>); Chinook Sciences; GM (cricket bats); Pedigree pet food; <a href="/w/index.php?title=VF_Cooperation&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="VF Cooperation (page does not exist)">VF Cooperation</a> (American clothing); <a href="/wiki/Changan_Automobile" title="Changan Automobile">Changan Automobile</a> (Chinese-made automobiles); the credit reference agency <a href="/wiki/Experian" title="Experian">Experian</a>; energy company <a href="/wiki/E.ON_UK" title="E.ON UK">E.ON UK</a>; betting company <a href="/wiki/Gala_Coral_Group" class="mw-redirect" title="Gala Coral Group">Gala Group</a>; amusement and gambling machine manufacturer <a href="/w/index.php?title=Bell-Fruit-Games&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Bell-Fruit-Games (page does not exist)">Bell-Fruit-Games</a>; engineering company <a href="/wiki/Siemens_AG" class="mw-redirect" title="Siemens AG">Siemens</a>; sportswear manufacturers <a href="/wiki/Speedo" title="Speedo">Speedo</a>; high-street opticians <a href="/wiki/Vision_Express" title="Vision Express">Vision Express</a> and <a href="/wiki/Specsavers" title="Specsavers">Specsavers</a>; games and publishing company <a href="/wiki/Games_Workshop" title="Games Workshop">Games Workshop</a>; PC software developer <a href="/wiki/Serif_Europe" title="Serif Europe">Serif Europe</a> (publisher of <a href="/wiki/PagePlus" title="PagePlus">PagePlus</a> and other titles); web hosting provider <a href="/wiki/Heart_Internet" title="Heart Internet">Heart Internet</a>; the American credit card company <a href="/wiki/Capital_One" title="Capital One">Capital One</a>; the national law firm <a href="/wiki/Browne_Jacobson" title="Browne Jacobson">Browne Jacobson</a>; and <a href="/wiki/Earache_Records" title="Earache Records">Earache Records</a>, an independent music company founded by local resident <a href="/wiki/Digby_Pearson" title="Digby Pearson">Digby Pearson</a>, based on Handel Street in <a href="/wiki/Sneinton" title="Sneinton">Sneinton</a>. Nottingham also has offices of <a href="/wiki/Nottingham_Building_Society" title="Nottingham Building Society">Nottingham Building Society</a> (established 1849); <a href="/wiki/HM_Revenue_and_Customs" title="HM Revenue and Customs">HM Revenue and Customs</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Driving_Standards_Agency" title="Driving Standards Agency">Driving Standards Agency</a>; <a href="/wiki/Ofsted" title="Ofsted">Ofsted</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Care_Quality_Commission" title="Care Quality Commission">Care Quality Commission</a>; and <a href="/wiki/BBC_East_Midlands" title="BBC East Midlands">BBC East Midlands</a>.
</p><p>Nottingham was named one of the UK's six science cities in 2005 by the then <a href="/wiki/Chancellor_of_the_Exchequer" title="Chancellor of the Exchequer">Chancellor of the Exchequer</a> <a href="/wiki/Gordon_Brown" title="Gordon Brown">Gordon Brown</a>. Among the science-based industries within the city is <a href="/wiki/BioCity_Nottingham" title="BioCity Nottingham">BioCity</a>. Founded as a joint venture between Nottingham Trent University and the University of Nottingham, it is the UK's biggest bioscience innovation and incubation centre, housing around 80 science-based companies.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
